The respawned process inherits only\\n26:     stdout/stderr from the current process.\\n27: \\n28:     Only a single respawn is allowed. ``respawn_module`` will fail on nested respawns. Modules are encouraged\\n29:     to call `has_respawned()` to defensively guide behavior before calling ``respawn_module``, and to ensure\\n30:     that the target interpreter exists, as ``respawn_module`` will not fail gracefully.\\n31: \\n32:     :arg interpreter_path: path to a Python interpreter to respawn the current module\\n33:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n34: \\n35:     if has_respawned():\\n36:         raise Exception('module has already been respawned')\\n37: \\n38:     # FUTURE: we need a safe way to log that a respawn has occurred for forensic/debug purposes\\n39:     payload = _create_payload()\\n40:     stdin_read, stdin_write = os.pipe()\\n41:     os.write(stdin_write, to_bytes(payload))\\n42:     os.close(stdin_write)\\n43:     rc = subprocess.call([interpreter_path, '--'], stdin=stdin_read)\\n44:     sys.exit(rc)  # pylint: disable=ansible-bad-function\\n45: \\n46: \\n47: def probe_interpreters_for_module(interpreter_paths, module_name):\\n48:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n49:     Probes a supplied list of Python interpreters, returning the first one capable of\\n50:     importing the named module. This is useful when attempting to locate a \\\"system\\n51:     Python\\\" where OS-packaged utility modules are located.\\n52: \\n53:     :arg interpreter_paths: iterable of paths to Python interpreters. The paths will be probed\\n54:     in order, and the first path that exists and can successfully import the named module will\\n55:     be returned (or ``None`` if probing fails for all supplied paths).\\n56:     :arg module_name: fully-qualified Python module name to probe for (eg, ``selinux``)\\n57:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n58:     for interpreter_path in interpreter_paths:\\n59:         if not os.path.exists(interpreter_path):\\n60:             continue\\n61:         try:\\n62:             rc = subprocess.call([interpreter_path, '-c', 'import {0}'.format(module_name)])\\n63:             if rc == 0:\\n64:                 return interpreter_path\\n65:         except Exception:\\n66:             continue\\n67: \\n68:     return None\\n69: \\n70: \\n71: def _create_payload():\\n72:     from ansible.module_utils import basic\\n73:     smuggled_args = getattr(basic, '_ANSIBLE_ARGS')\\n74:     if not smuggled_args:\\n75:         raise Exception('unable to access ansible.module_utils.basic._ANSIBLE_ARGS (not launched by AnsiballZ?)')\\n76:     module_fqn = sys.modules['__main__']._module_fqn\\n77:     modlib_path = sys.modules['__main__']._modlib_path\\n78: b\\\"\\\"\\\"{smuggled_args}\\\"\\\"\\\".strip()\\n85: \\n86: \\n87: if __name__ == '__main__':\\n88:     sys.path.insert(0, modlib_path)\\n89: \\n90:     from ansible.module_utils import basic\\n91:     basic._ANSIBLE_ARGS = smuggled_args\\n92: \\n93:     runpy.run_module(module_fqn, init_globals=dict(_respawned=True), run_name='__main__', alter_sys=True)\\n94:     '''\\n95: \\n96:     respawn_code = respawn_code_template.format(module_fqn=module_fqn, modlib_path=modlib_path, smuggled_args=to_native(smuggled_args))\\n97: \\n98:     return respawn_code\\n99: def has_respawned():\n15:     return hasattr(sys.modules['__main__'], '_respawned')\n16: \n17: \n18: def respawn_module(interpreter_path):\n19:     \"\"\"\n20:     Respawn the currently-running Ansible Python module under the specified Python interpreter.\n21: \n22:     Ansible modules that require libraries that are typically available only under well-known interpreters\n23:     (eg, ``yum``, ``apt``, ``dnf``) can use bespoke logic to determine the libraries they need are not\n24:     available, then call `respawn_module` to re-execute the current module under a different interpreter\n25:     and exit the current process when the new subprocess has completed. The respawned process inherits only\n26:     stdout/stderr from the current process.\n27: \n28:     Only a single respawn is allowed. ``respawn_module`` will fail on nested respawns. Modules are encouraged\n29:     to call `has_respawned()` to defensively guide behavior before calling ``respawn_module``, and to ensure\n30:     that the target interpreter exists, as ``respawn_module`` will not fail gracefully.\n31: \n32:     :arg interpreter_path: path to a Python interpreter to respawn the current module\n33:     \"\"\"\n34: \n35:     if has_respawned():\n36:         raise Exception('module has already been respawned')\n37: \n38:     # FUTURE: we need a safe way to log that a respawn has occurred for forensic/debug purposes\n39:     payload = _create_payload()\n40:     stdin_read, stdin_write = os.pipe()\n41:     os.write(stdin_write, to_bytes(payload))\n42:     os.close(stdin_write)\n43:     rc = subprocess.call([interpreter_path, '--'], stdin=stdin_read)\n44:     sys.exit(rc)  # pylint: disable=ansible-bad-function\n45: \n46: \n47: def probe_interpreters_for_module(interpreter_paths, module_name):\n48:     \"\"\"\n49:     Probes a supplied list of Python interpreters, returning the first one capable of\n50:     importing the named module. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01E3RakbTSoa1EtbHJ6YPF4M","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Copyright: (c) 2021, Ansible Project\n     2→# GNU General Public License v3.0+ (see COPYING or https://www.gnu.org/licenses/gpl-3.0.txt)\n     3→\n     4→from __future__ import (absolute_import, division, print_function)\n     5→__metaclass__ = type\n     6→\n     7→import os\n     8→import subprocess\n     9→import sys\n    10→\n    11→from ansible.module_utils.common.text.converters import to_bytes, to_native\n    12→\n    13→\n    14→def has_respawned():\n    15→    return hasattr(sys.modules['__main__'], '_respawned')\n    16→\n    17→\n    18→def respawn_module(interpreter_path):\n    19→    \"\"\"\n    20→    Respawn the currently-running Ansible Python module under the specified Python interpreter.\n    21→\n    22→    Ansible modules that require libraries that are typically available only under well-known interpreters\n    23→    (eg, ``yum``, ``apt``, ``dnf``) can use bespoke logic to determine the libraries they need are not\n    24→    available, then call `respawn_module` to re-execute the current module under a different interpreter\n    25→    and exit the current process when the new subprocess has completed. The respawned process inherits only\n    26→    stdout/stderr from the current process.\n    27→\n    28→    Only a single respawn is allowed. ``respawn_module`` will fail on nested respawns. Modules are encouraged\n    29→    to call `has_respawned()` to defensively guide behavior before calling ``respawn_module``, and to ensure\n    30→    that the target interpreter exists, as ``respawn_module`` will not fail gracefully.\n    31→\n    32→    :arg interpreter_path: path to a Python interpreter to respawn the current module\n    33→    \"\"\"\n    34→\n    35→    if has_respawned():\n    36→        raise Exception('module has already been respawned')\n    37→\n    38→    # FUTURE: we need a safe way to log that a respawn has occurred for forensic/debug purposes\n    39→    payload = _create_payload()\n    40→    stdin_read, stdin_write = os.pipe()\n    41→    os.write(stdin_write, to_bytes(payload))\n    42→    os.close(stdin_write)\n    43→    rc = subprocess.call([interpreter_path, '--'], stdin=stdin_read)\n    44→    sys.exit(rc)  # pylint: disable=ansible-bad-function\n    45→\n    46→\n    47→def probe_interpreters_for_module(interpreter_paths, module_name):\n    48→    \"\"\"\n    49→    Probes a supplied list of Python interpreters, returning the first one capable of\n    50→    importing the named module. This is useful when attempting to locate a \"system\n    51→    Python\" where OS-packaged utility modules are located.\n    52→\n    53→    :arg interpreter_paths: iterable of paths to Python interpreters. The paths will be probed\n    54→    in order, and the first path that exists and can successfully import the named module will\n    55→    be returned (or ``None`` if probing fails for all supplied The respawned process inherits only\n    stdout/stderr from the current process.\n\n    Only a single respawn is allowed. ``respawn_module`` will fail on nested respawns. Modules are encouraged\n    to call `has_respawned()` to defensively guide behavior before calling ``respawn_module``, and to ensure\n    that the target interpreter exists, as ``respawn_module`` will not fail gracefully.\n\n    :arg interpreter_path: path to a Python interpreter to respawn the current module\n    \"\"\"\n\n    if has_respawned():\n        raise Exception('module has already been respawned')\n\n    # FUTURE: we need a safe way to log that a respawn has occurred for forensic/debug purposes\n    payload = _create_payload()\n    stdin_read, stdin_write = os.pipe()\n    os.write(stdin_write, to_bytes(payload))\n    os.close(stdin_write)\n    rc = subprocess.call([interpreter_path, '--'], stdin=stdin_read)\n    sys.exit(rc)  # pylint: disable=ansible-bad-function\n\n\ndef probe_interpreters_for_module(interpreter_paths, module_name):\n    \"\"\"\n    Probes a supplied list of Python interpreters, returning the first one capable of\n    importing the named module. This is useful when attempting to locate a \"system\n    Python\" where OS-packaged utility modules are located.\n\n    :arg interpreter_paths: iterable of paths to Python interpreters. The paths will be probed\n    in order, and the first path that exists and can successfully import the named module will\n    be returned (or ``None`` if probing fails for all supplied paths).\n    :arg module_name: fully-qualified Python module name to probe for (eg, ``selinux``)\n    \"\"\"\n    for interpreter_path in interpreter_paths:\n        if not os.path.exists(interpreter_path):\n            continue\n        try:\n            rc = subprocess.call([interpreter_path, '-c', 'import {0}'.format(module_name)])\n            if rc == 0:\n                return interpreter_path\n        except Exception:\n            continue\n\n    return None\n\n\ndef _create_payload():\n    from ansible.module_utils import basic\n    smuggled_args = getattr(basic, '_ANSIBLE_ARGS')\n    if not smuggled_args:\n        raise Exception('unable to access ansible.module_utils.basic._ANSIBLE_ARGS (not launched by AnsiballZ?)')\n    module_fqn = sys.modules['__main__']._module_fqn\n    modlib_path = sys.modules['__main__']._modlib_path\n    respawn_code_template = '''\nimport runpy\nimport sys\n\nmodule_fqn = '{module_fqn}'\nmodlib_path = '{modlib_path}'\nsmuggled_args = b\"\"\"{smuggled_args}\"\"\".strip()\n\n\nif __name__ == '__main__':\n    sys.path.insert(0, modlib_path)\n\n    from ansible.module_utils import basic\n    basic._ANSIBLE_ARGS = smuggled_args\n\n    runpy.run_module(module_fqn, init_globals=dict(_respawned=True), run_name='__main__', alter_sys=True)\n    '''\n\n    respawn_code = respawn_code_template.format(module_fqn=module_fqn, modlib_path=modlib_path, smuggled_args=to_native(smuggled_args))\n\n    return respawn_code\n","numLines":99,"startLine":1,"totalLines":99}}}

Let me check if there's an issue with the respawn payload. Let me examine the respawn_code_template more carefully:\n\n```python\nrespawn_code_template = '''\nimport runpy\nimport sys\n\nmodule_fqn = '{module_fqn}'\nmodlib_path = '{modlib_path}'\nsmuggled_args = b\"\"\"{smuggled_args}\"\"\".strip()\n\n\nif __name__ == '__main__':\n    sys.path.insert(0, modlib_path)\n\n    from ansible.module_utils import basic\n    basic._ANSIBLE_ARGS = smuggled_args\n\n    runpy.run_module(module_fqn, init_globals=dict(_respawned=True), run_name='__main__', alter_sys=True)\n    '''\n```\n\nThe issue I see is that when the respawned code runs, it needs to have the `_module_fqn` and `_modlib_path` attributes set on sys.modules['__main__'] so that if the respawned module calls respawn_module again, it will have these attributes available.\n\nBut looking at the code, the respawned process uses `runpy.run_module`, which creates a new __main__ module. \n\nThe init_globals only include `_respawned=True`, not `_module_fqn` and `_modlib_path`. This means if the respawned module tries to respawn again, it will fail with AttributeError. The respawned process inherits only\n    stdout/stderr from the current process.\n\n    Only a single respawn is allowed. ``respawn_module`` will fail on nested respawns. Modules are encouraged\n    to call `has_respawned()` to defensively guide behavior before calling ``respawn_module``, and to ensure\n    that the target interpreter exists, as ``respawn_module`` will not fail gracefully.\n\n    :arg interpreter_path: path to a Python interpreter to respawn the current module\n    \"\"\"\n\n    if has_respawned():\n        raise Exception('module has already been respawned')\n\n    # FUTURE: we need a safe way to log that a respawn has occurred for forensic/debug purposes\n    payload = _create_payload()\n    stdin_read, stdin_write = os.pipe()\n    os.write(stdin_write, to_bytes(payload))\n    os.close(stdin_write)\n    rc = subprocess.call([interpreter_path, '--'], stdin=stdin_read)\n    sys.exit(rc)  # pylint: disable=ansible-bad-function\n\n\ndef probe_interpreters_for_module(interpreter_paths, module_name):\n    \"\"\"\n    Probes a supplied list of Python interpreters, returning the first one capable of\n    importing the named module. This is useful when attempting to locate a \"system\n    Python\" where OS-packaged utility modules are located.\n\n    :arg interpreter_paths: iterable of paths to Python interpreters.
